        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297"><meta property="og:title" content="Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/acsomega.4c00297.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Herein, we report a general route for the uniform coating of hard carbon (HC) powders via fluidized bed chemical vapor deposition. Carbon-based fine powders are excellent substrate materials for many catalytic and electrochemical applications but intrinsically difficult to fluidize and prone to elutriation. The reactor was designed to achieve as much retention of powders as possible, supported by a computational fluid dynamics study to assess the hydrodynamic behavior for varying gaseous flow rates. Solutions of the tin seleno- and thio-ether complexes [SnCl4{nBuSe(CH2)3SenBu}] and [SnCl4{nBuS(CH2)3SnBu}] were used as single source precursors and injected at high temperature into a fluidized bed of HC powders under nitrogen flow. The method allowed for the synthesis of HC-SnSx–SnSe2 composites at the gram scale with potential applications in electrocatalysis and sodium-ion battery anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/acsomega.4c00297.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '0842d6f1-dbc3-459a-a674-d11d2dcc3fc4'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

src="https://script.hotjar.com/modules.f98993dba31a932bc912.js"></script><script id="yui__dyn_0" type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a class="figure-viewer_image"><a href="#" title="Next" class="figure-viewer_next icon-angle-right" style="display: inline;"></a><a href="#" title="Previous" class="figure-viewer_prev icon-angle-left" <script nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Fluidized Bed Chemical Vapor Deposition on Hard Carbon Powders to Produce Composite Energy Materials", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.4c00297", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Herein, we report a general route for the uniform coating of hard carbon (HC) powders via fluidized bed chemical vapor deposition. Carbon-based fine powders are excellent substrate materials for many catalytic and electrochemical applications but intrinsically difficult to fluidize and prone to elutriation. The reactor was designed to achieve as much retention of powders as possible, supported by a computational fluid dynamics study to assess the hydrodynamic behavior for varying gaseous flow rates. Solutions of the tin seleno- and thio-ether complexes [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] and [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] were used as single source precursors and injected at high temperature into a fluidized bed of HC powders under nitrogen flow. The method allowed for the synthesis of HC-SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub>–SnSe<sub>2</sub> composites at the gram scale with potential applications in electrocatalysis and sodium-ion battery anodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under</p><div class="article__cc-license__inter"><div class="article__cc-license--hoverable"><a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-text" class="link--bold" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License">CC-BY 4.0
                                    <span class="arrow"></span></a>
                                .

                                            article in any medium or format and to <span class="bold">adapt</span>(remix,

                                                actual license. It is not a license and has no legal value. Carefully
                                                review the actual license before using these materials.
                                            </p></div></div></div></div><ul class="article__cc-license__list"><li class="article__cc-license__item"><a aria-describedby="tooltip-cc-0" class="article__cc-license__link" data-id="article_abstract_CC-License_IconCC"><span class="arrow"></span><img src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/cc-license/cc.svg" alt="cc licence" width="22" height="22" class="article__cc-license__icon"></a><div role="tooltip" id="tooltip-cc-0" class="article__hover" data-id="article_abstract_CC-IconTooltip"><div class="article__tooltip"><h3>License Summary*</h3><p>You are free to <span class="bold">share</span>(copy and redistribute)

Published by American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292088&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical vapor deposition"> Chemical vapor deposition </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290743&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Deposition"> Deposition </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291553&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Granular materials"> Granular materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290865&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nitrogen"> Nitrogen </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291087&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Precursors"> Precursors </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Composite materials are fundamental for many applications in energy storage, energy conversion, and catalysis. The combination of cheap, environmentally friendly carbon-based materials and inorganic materials with controllable catalytic, electronic, and optical properties is a winning strategy to fabricate versatile materials for energy applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Carbon materials are common substrates for the fabrication of heterogeneous catalysts, as by surface modification they can stabilize nanoparticles and achieve tunable catalyst–support interactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Carbon-supported semiconductors are key materials for advanced photoelectrocatalysis, with important implications in the removal of pollutants from water and for energy conversion applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> In these composites, the formation of a heterojunction can greatly enhance electron–hole pair separation and promote electron transfer over recombination mechanisms. Carbon composites are also of great interest for battery electrodes, as highly conductive supports with a large surface-to-volume ratio on which other active materials can be deposited, while the carbon itself can also provide capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this study, we focused on the synthesis of tin chalcogenide (SnE<sub><i>x</i></sub>) composites with HC powders, which could be extended to several material combinations. Tin chalcogenides (SnE<sub><i>x</i></sub>) are semiconductor materials of particular importance for several technological applications in modern electronics. Their layered structure with weak van der Waals interactions between planes makes them structural analogues with graphene. Interestingly, the band gap of the material can be changed by choosing appropriate chalcogens or their combination with tunable band gaps in the 1–2 eV range. On the other hand, weakly coupled layers allow for confined charge, spin, and heat transfer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 5 to 13">(5−13)</a> In this respect, tin chalcogenides hold a high potential for several applications in electronics, such as optoelectronics, thermoelectrics, and phase change devices, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref10 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref10 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 6, 10 and 14 to 19">(6,10,14−19)</a> and in photocatalysis and electrocatalysis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> In particular, they are a high capacity active material for Na-ion battery anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> Hence, the deposition of metal dichalcogenides with controlled composition, good substrate/deposit contact, and uniform dispersion on HC powders can be used to exemplify depositions onto carbons to produce functional materials.</div><div class="NLM_p">The most common synthetic methods for carbon-based materials-inorganic composites are wet-chemical methods, including hydrothermal deposition, incipient wetness impregnation, and coprecipitation, which are limited in the types of materials, which can be deposited and may not provide uniform, well contacted layers on the surface of the particles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Chemical vapor deposition (CVD) has significant advantages for the deposition of semiconductors, but its use to treat powders, such as carbon materials, is still particularly challenging. CVD can often be used to form coatings with high adherence to the substrate but may not produce uniform coatings on powders. Precursors may not penetrate a layer of substrate particles in a conventional CVD geometry, and the coating uniformity can be affected. An effective way to overcome this limitation is plug flow-CVD, in which the powders are not packed but slightly spread over a small area, allowing for the precursor to be more uniformly adsorbed. This could be an effective method to treat small amounts of powders with several materials and controlled loading, but it is not easily scalable to the treatment of grams of powders. The use of fluidized beds of particles is more scalable and has been widely used in industrial coating and treatment processes. The particles are suspended on a porous plate (distributor) in the reactor bed, and a flow of gas from the bottom of the plate is increased until the particles move as a fluid. In such fluidized regimes, the particle surface areas are exposed during the process and uniform coatings or treatments can be achieved. The ability of particles to be fluidized is described by the Geldart classification, showing that only particles with greater size (tens to hundred μm) and density, thus belonging to the A and B groups, can be stably fluidized. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> For this reason, FB-CVD has found important industrial applications for, and has been limited to, the treatment of particles such as silica, sand, alumina, stainless steel, metals, and metal carbides, which consist of coarse granules of hundreds of micrometers in diameter and dense material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 30 to 39">(30−39)</a> The fluidization of finer particles (particle diameter smaller than 20 μm) on the other hand should have several advantages in terms of lower fluidization velocity required and higher particle to gas and surface-to-bed heat transfer and mass transfer rate; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> nonetheless, fluidization is dominated by cohesive interactions, making a smooth fluidization more difficult to achieve. For finer particles with &lt; 20 μm diameter belonging to the group C of the Geldart classification, van der Waals interactions dominate the hydrodynamic forces in the fluidized bed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 to 29">(27−29)</a> Agglomerates form in situ, broadening the actual size dispersion of the particles, so that they do not fluidize smoothly, and gas channeling through the bed takes place. At the higher gas velocities required to fluidize the agglomerates, finer particles can more easily be elutriated and escape the reactor. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Therefore, despite its potential, CVD on a fluidized bed of fine powders is made more difficult and requires more ingenuity in reactor design to avoid particle agglomeration and entrainment.</div><div class="NLM_p">In this contribution, we developed two methods for the deposition of inorganic semiconductor coatings of tin dichalcogenides on HC fine powders, based on plug flow- and FB-CVD. The fluidization of HC fine powders was achieved by optimizing the FB-CVD reactor design in order to minimize the particles entrainment. A custom-built bulb-shaped quartz reactor was designed to optimize the CVD from precursors in the vapor phase. The single-source precursors [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] and [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> were dissolved in a volatile solvent, and the solution was injected directly in the fluidized hard carbon powders bed at high temperature, with the advantage of having high flows of precursor compared to a gaseous counterpart, whose feed rate would be limited by the saturation capacity of the carrier gas. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As a consequence, reaction times could be reduced, preventing the attrition of the deposited layers. Composites formed in all of the conditions explored, but the quartz reactor design and the injection conditions had to be optimized to retain the fine powders, which are subject to agglomeration and entrainment, in the reactor and maximize the deposition. Changes in the reactor design played a key role in optimizing powder retention and producing composites with a uniform composition. This study on HC powders could be extended to other C-based support materials and inorganic nanomaterials and pave the way for a more generalized method for the synthesis of powder composites.</div><div class="NLM_p last">It is worth mentioning that FB-CVD has so far been used for the synthesis of metals, alloys (FeAl, NiAl), metal oxides, such as SiO<sub>2</sub>, SnO<sub>2</sub>, ZrO<sub>2</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref36" aria-label="reference 30 to 34 and 36">(30−34,36)</a> carbides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> and nitrides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> To the best of our knowledge, this is the first example of FB-CVD of metal chalcogenide semiconductors. The use of single source precursors was crucial for the deposition of uniform thin films dichalcogenides with controlled composition and stoichiometry, since once the complex dissociates at high temperature both Sn and either Se/S become available in a specific ratio, and their reactivity is spatially resolved. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> We propose that this method could be further extended to other semiconductors and their combination to form materials with a tunable band gap and energy density.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">Hard carbon powders Carbotron P(J) were provided by Kureha Battery Materials Japan Co., Ltd. Before use, they were dried at 70 °C for 24 h and 120 °C for 2 h to eliminate adsorbed moisture. The solvents used (THF, toluene, and hexane) were dried by distillation from sodium wire prior to use.</div><div class="NLM_p"><b>[SnCl</b><sub><b>4</b></sub><b>{</b><sup><b>n</b></sup><b>BuSe(CH</b><sub><b>2</b></sub><b>)</b><sub><b>3</b></sub><b>Se</b><sup><b>n</b></sup><b>Bu}]</b> precursor was prepared according to de Groot et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Selenium granules were ground into a fine powder under nitrogen atmosphere for 5 min and loaded in a Schlenk tube under inert atmosphere together with 25 mL of anhydrous THF and a magnetic stirrer bar. The tube was connected to a Schlenk line to be purged under a nitrogen atmosphere and placed on a stirring plate and into a liquid nitrogen bath to freeze the solution. A dry solution of <sup><i>n</i></sup>BuLi in hexanes (15 mL, 2.43 × 10<sup>–2</sup>mol) was then injected dropwise through a septum. The liquid nitrogen bath was removed, and the system was allowed to stir. Further, 1–2 mL <sup><i>n</i></sup>BuLi solution was added dropwise until a transparent pale-yellow solution was obtained, indicating that all the Se had reacted. The solution was allowed to stir at room temperature for 1 h. A total of 1.5 mL of 1,3-dichloropropane was added dropwise under stirring at room temperature and allowed to stir for 2 h. The solution was concentrated in vacuo, washed in hexane (15 mL), and filtered to eliminate the LiCl precipitate. The filtered solution was dried in vacuo to recover a pale-yellow oil which was stored in a N<sub>2</sub>-filled glovebox.</div><div class="NLM_p">For the precursor synthesis, 0.9 g of SnCl<sub>4</sub> was loaded in a Schlenk tube under inert atmosphere and dissolved in 15 mL of anhydrous hexane. A solution of 1.06 g of <sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu dissolved in 10 mL of dry hexane was injected dropwise in the SnCl<sub>4</sub> solution while stirring for 30 min. The precursor was then washed with hexane, filtered, and dried in vacuo to eliminate all the solvent. The powder (yield ≈ 60%) was collected and stored in a nitrogen-filled glovebox.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><b>[SnCl</b><sub><b>4</b></sub><b>{</b><sup><b>n</b></sup><b>BuS(CH</b><sub><b>2</b></sub><b>)</b><sub><b>3</b></sub><b>S</b><sup><b>n</b></sup><b>Bu}]</b> was synthesized according to Gurnani et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> For the precursor synthesis, a solution of 1 mmol of ligand in 5 mL of anhydrous hexane was added slowly to a solution of SnCl<sub>4</sub> 1.25 mmol in anhydrous hexane (10 mL) under constant stirring at room temperature and under a dinitrogen atmosphere. After 20 min, the white precipitate was dried in vacuo and stored in a glovebox.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Low Pressure-CVD (LP-CVD)</h3><div class="NLM_p">Precursor powders (30 mg) were loaded in a nitrogen filled glovebox at the closed end of a quartz tube together with Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> (500 nm) substrate tiles (each 20 × 8 × 1 mm), which were positioned adjacently along the tube at regular space intervals from the precursor. Two glass valves at both ends of the tube allowed it to be closed under a dinitrogen atmosphere before moving it to a Schlenk line.</div><div class="NLM_p">The tube was then placed in the middle of a tubular furnace, the precursor being initially just out of the furnace and connected to a Schlenk line. The system was kept under a vacuum until the pressure stabilized at 0.2 mmHg. Quartz wool was placed at both ends of the furnace to secure a stable temperature inside the furnace. The furnace temperature was then set to the desired temperature. Once the temperature was stable, the tube was moved inside the furnace so that the bulb, containing the precursor, would be inside the furnace. The system was kept at high temperature for 30 min and successively taken out of the furnace to cool fast. When at room temperature, it was transferred into a glovebox.</div><div class="NLM_p last">A temperature profiling was accomplished with a separate probe to register the actual substrate temperature in different zones of the furnace.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Plug Flow-CVD</h3><div class="NLM_p">A sketch of the setup is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. HC powders (80 mg) were loaded in an open quartz tube adjacent to a disk of sintered quartz. The solid precursor, in the form of a powder (30 mg), was loaded in a boat in a nitrogen filled glovebox and placed at the other end of the quartz tube. Two valves at opposite ends of the tube were closed to secure the tube under a nitrogen atmosphere. The tube was then adjusted in the middle of a tubular furnace so that HC powders were in the heating zone and the precursor would be out of the furnace. The tube was connected by one end to a Schlenk line and by the other to a bubbler. Nitrogen was allowed to flow for a few minutes, and the furnace was heated to the reaction temperature, i.e., 400, 450, 500, and 600 °C, in the respective experiments. Once the temperature was stabilized, the tube was moved in such a way that both the precursor and HC substrate were in the heating zone of the furnace. After 30 min at high temperature, the tube was moved out of the furnace and allowed to cool down. Once at room temperature, the tube was brought to a reduced pressure and transferred in a glovebox, where the powders were collected and stored. The HC-precursor distance was also adjusted to regulate the precursor evaporation temperature and the deposition temperature.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Sketch of the plug flow-CVD setup; (b) sketch of FB-CVD reactor; (c) picture of the FB-CVD setup.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> FB-CVD</h3><div class="NLM_p">A sketch and a picture of the setup, prior to modifications, are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,c. In a typical experiment, 0.2 g of precursor was loaded in a Schlenk tube under a flow of nitrogen and dissolved in 12 mL of anhydrous toluene by slightly heating to 50 °C under stirring for 10 min. A total of 0.8–1 g of hard carbon powders (average diameter 10 μm) was loaded on the FB-CVD reactor distributor plate, and the nitrogen flows were adjusted to 0.3 L min<sup>−1</sup>. Under these conditions, the powders were slightly moving on the plate, but a full fluidization was not achieved. The temperature was increased by 20 °C/min up to 450 °C, with the bulb of the reactor being in the middle of the heating zone of the tubular furnace. Once the temperature was stabilized, the nitrogen flows were increased to 0.5−0.8 min<sup>−1</sup> to achieve a full fluidization of the powders. The air-free precursor solution was withdrawn with a syringe and injected dropwise (1.3 mL min<sup>−1</sup>) with the aid of a syringe pump through a septum directly in the CVD zone. The system was kept at high temperature for an additional 30 min after all the precursor was injected and then allowed to cool to room temperature. Alternatively, for a multiinjection process, a second injection of precursor was performed in the same conditions, then kept steady for 30 min before cooling down.</div><div class="NLM_p last">When at room temperature the reactor was closed under nitrogen and transported to a nitrogen-filled glovebox, where the powders were collected.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Characterization Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p"><sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra were measured from solutions in anhydrous CDCl<sub>3</sub> on a Bruker AV400 spectrometer. The analytical data are the following.</div><div class="NLM_p">[SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup>n</sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup>n</sup>Bu}] (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). <sup>1</sup>H NMR (CDCl<sub>3</sub>, 298 K) δ/ppm: 0.92 (t, [6H], CH<sub>3</sub>), 1.48 (m, [4H], CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>), 1.75 (m, [4H], CH<sub>2</sub>), 2.45 (m, [2H], –SeCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>Se−), 3.22 (m, [4H], CH<sub>2</sub>Se), 3.32 (m, [4H], SeCH<sub>2</sub>). <sup>13</sup>C{<sup>1</sup>H} NMR (CDCl<sub>3</sub>) δ/ppm: 13.41 (CH<sub>3</sub>), 22.83 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 25.02 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 30.05 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 32.9 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 35.58 (CH<sub>2</sub>).</div><div class="NLM_p">[SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup>n</sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup>n</sup>Bu}] (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). <sup>1</sup>H NMR (CDCl<sub>3</sub>, 298 K) δ/ppm: 0.95 (t, [6H], CH<sub>3</sub>), 1.43 (m, [4H], CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>3</sub>), 1.72 (m, [4H], CH<sub>2</sub>), 2.31 (m, [2H], –SCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>S−), 3.07 (m, [4H], SCH<sub>2</sub>), 3.26 (m, [4H], SCH<sub>2</sub>). <sup>13</sup>C{<sup>1</sup>H} NMR (CDCl<sub>3</sub>) δ/ppm: 13.70 (CH<sub>3</sub>), 21.77 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 29.40 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 34.19 (CH<sub>2</sub>), 36.74 (CH<sub>2</sub>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were collected by using a Rigaku SmartLab system. Samples synthesized by LP-CVD on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> substrates were studied in grazing incidence geometry with X-ray Cu Kα (λ= 1.5418 Å) and a Hypix 2D detector. HC-based samples synthesized by plug-flow and FB-CVD were analyzed by capillary transmission. XRD of the TGA residue powders was measured with a Bruker D2 Phaser instrument using Cu Kα radiation. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was conducted using a Philips XL-30 ESEM (20 kV accelerating voltage) with a ThermoFisher Ultradry energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) detector and Noran System 7 data processing. A ZEISS sigma FE-SEM was used for the imaging and elemental mapping of multimaterial composites (HC-SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub>–SnSe<sub>2</sub>). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) used a Netzch TG 209 F1 Libra instrument with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> up to 1100 °C under 20 mL min<sup>–1</sup> of oxygen and 30 mL min<sup>–1</sup> of argon and a protective Ar flow of 20 mL min<sup>–1</sup>. The BET surface area and porosity were analyzed by nitrogen physisorption with a BET-TriStar II 3020.</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> CFD Study</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The geometry of the reactor was designed with SolidWorks. The HC powders were approximated to spherical C particles with a density of 1.5 g cm<sup>–3</sup> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and average particle size of 10 μm, as obtained from a size statistics measured by SEM images with an Image J software.</div></div><div id="sec2_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Governing Equations</h3><div class="NLM_p">The kinetic theory of granular flow (KTGF) model within Ansys Fluent 2021 R2 was used to model the interactions between gas and solid particles within this fluidized bed. This model considers two different phases in one controlled volume by applying a volume fraction variable and treats them as an interpenetrating continuum. The carbon solid phase was assumed to consist of spherical particles of the same diameter of 10 μm. Each phase was solved individually in accordance with the mass and momentum conservation equations, as presented in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. The gas–solid interphase exchange coefficient, <i>K</i><sub>gs</sub>, was modeled using the Syamlal–O’Brien drag model. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> No reactions were considered in the CFD model, and the inlet temperatures and reactor temperatures are assumed to have reached an isothermal temperature of 430 °C. The gaseous phase properties were taken accordingly at that temperature, and the energy equation is neglected.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">(13)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">&nbsp;</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">(15)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The kinetic fluctuations between particles were considered using the kinetic theory of granular flow given in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. A full review of the equation derivation is provided by Gidaspow. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The solid shear viscosity, μ<sub>c</sub>, is composed of collisional, μ<sub>c,&nbsp;col</sub>, kinetic, μ<sub>c,&nbsp;kin</sub>, and frictional, μ<sub>c,&nbsp;fr</sub>, interactions. For highly dense flows, such as those experienced by Geldart C particles, the frictional viscosity is applied as the volume fraction for the particles approaching the maximum packing limit. The frictional model of the Schaeffer expression <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> was used to model the frictional viscosity in the present case. The bulk viscosity, λ<sub>c</sub>, accounts for the resistance of particle to expansion and depression and was calculated using an expression from Lun et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The solid pressure, <i>p</i><sub>c</sub>, considers both kinetic and collisional contributions and evolves from an equation of state that is analogous to the van der Waals equation of state for gases. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The radial distribution function, <i>g</i><sub>0</sub>, modifies the probability of particle collisions as the phase approaches the maximum packing limit. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a> provides an overview of the constitutive equations used in the present case.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i34"> Boundary and Initial Conditions</h4><div class="NLM_p">The particle bed is initially set to a height of 0.01 m above the quartz disc distributor. The particles were assigned a diameter, <i>d</i><sub>c</sub> = 10 μm, and density, ρ<sub>c</sub> = 1520 kg m<sup>–3</sup>. The inlet provided a continual supply of nitrogen with cases considering velocities ranging from 0.1 m s<sup>–1</sup> &lt; <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0" style=""><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" style="width: 1.651em; top: -2.213em; left: 0em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-2"><span class="msub" id="MathJax-Span-3"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.48em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute;
