clip: rect(3.355em, 1000.46em, 4.151em, -999.997em); top: -3.974em; left: 0em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-5" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Italic;">𝜈<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.031em; left: 0.116em;"><span style="height: 0em; vertical-align: 0em; width: 0.457em; display: inline-block; overflow: hidden;"></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-6" style="font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">⃗&nbsp;<span style="height: 0em; vertical-align: 0em; margin-left: -0.224em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0em;"></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.804em; left: 0.571em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-7"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-8" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">N</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-9" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: STIXGeneral-Regular;">2</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 3.98em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.219em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.247em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.191em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="inline"><msub><mover><mi>ν</mi><mo accent="true" stretchy="false">→</mo></mover><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></math></script> &lt; 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup> to reflect a flow rate spanning 0.5–1.0 L min<sup>–1</sup>. A pressure outlet was used and set to atmospheric pressure. The particle–wall collisions significantly impact the shear stress on the walls of the bulb reactor. No-slip boundary conditions were assigned for the gas phase tangential and normal velocities, whereas a tangential slip condition is imposed for the particulate phase which was developed by Johnson and Jackson. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The near-wall granular temperature considers the granular temperature flux normal to the wall and to the energy dissipation due to particle-wall collisions. A breakdown of the gaseous and particulate physical properties is provided in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>, along with key closure parameters to support the collisional equations provided in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Tables 1</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a>.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. Table of Parameters</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">&nbsp;</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><b>parameter</b></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center"><b>value</b></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="3" scope="row" align="left"><b>Gas: Nitrogen</b></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_m023.gif" alt="" id="_i37"></span></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">velocity</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.1&nbsp;–&nbsp;0.2&nbsp;m&nbsp;s<sup>–1</sup> (∼0.5&nbsp;−1.0&nbsp;L&nbsp;min<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">ρ<sub><i>N</i>2</sub>&nbsp;(@&nbsp;450&nbsp;°C)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">density</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.469 kg m<sup>–3</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">μ<sub>N2</sub> (@ 450 °C)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">shear viscosity</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">3.283 kg m<sup>–1</sup> s<sup>–1</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="3" scope="row" align="left"><b>Particle: Carbon</b></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">ρ<sub>c</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">particle density</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">1520 kg m<sup>–3</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>d</i><sub>c</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">particle diameter</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">10 μm</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>e</i></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">particle coefficient of restitution</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.95</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left"><i>e</i><sub>w</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">wall coefficient of restitution</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">0.95</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec2_7_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i38" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i38"> Numerical Simulations</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The finite volume method was used to solve the governing equations. The coupling and correction of the velocity and pressure was carried out using the Phase Coupled SIMPLE (PC-SIMPLE) algorithm. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The discretization of the convective terms was performed by using the second-order upwind scheme. An unsteady simulation was performed with a time step of 1 × 10<sup>–4</sup> s to ensure fast convergence with 30 iterations per time step. The convergence criterion between two iterations was set to 1 × 10<sup>–3</sup>.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i39">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i40" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i40"> Optimization of CVD Conditions</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To test the optimal conditions for the deposition of tin dichalcogenides, we carried out LP-CVD from tin seleno- and thio-ether complexes on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> substrates at different temperatures. In each experiment, several substrates could be placed in different positions of the furnace, i.e. experiencing different temperatures, so that it was possible to establish the optimal deposition temperature. Deposition from [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] at temperatures of 400 °C and higher produced SnSe<sub>2</sub> coatings consisting of randomly oriented hexagonal crystallites whose structure is consistent with bulk SnSe<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, top panels). SnS<sub>2</sub> coatings were deposited from [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] at temperatures in the range of 310–350 °C. In the latter experiments, the precursor was heated to temperatures as high as 650 °C, with the deposition taking place in the colder zone of the furnace, indicating that faster precursor evaporation produced uniform crystalline SnS<sub>2</sub> coatings (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, bottom panels). The coating composition was confirmed by EDS spectra acquired in correlation with SEM, measured at different locations on the thin films surface, showing ratios of Sn to S close to 1:2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i41" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i41"> Tin Dichalcogenide-HC Composites by Plug Flow-CVD</h3><div class="NLM_p">A different configuration was then used to deposit SnSe<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> on hard carbon powders by plug flow-CVD under a dinitrogen flow (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a). These experiments aimed at determining the best conditions for CVD on HC powder substrates under dinitrogen flow ambient pressure. The samples obtained after CVD from [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] were analyzed by XRD and SEM/EDS. All samples showed a high yield of deposited tin chalcogenide, but its distribution was uneven. While some particles of the powders were completely coated with tin chalcogenide platelets, some others were completely free of any sign of SnSe<sub>2</sub>. Sample homogeneity could be improved to a certain extent by using small amounts of HC (80 mg) and spreading the powder on the tube surface to allow most of the HC grains’ surface to be exposed to the precursor vapors. Overall, samples obtained in the deposition temperature in the 380–450 °C range gave the best results in terms of amount and homogeneity of SnSe<sub>2</sub> deposited on HC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). Slower precursor evaporation, achieved by a larger distance of the precursor source from the furnace hot zone, increased the amount of deposited material.</div><div class="NLM_p">Plug-flow CVD from [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] on HC exhibited greater sensitivity to temperature than LP-CVD. Samples obtained at deposition temperatures in the range of 300–450 °C showed the presence of SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub> species deposited on the surface of HC (SEM-EDS), but XRD analysis revealed a poor crystallinity. By increasing the furnace temperature to 600 °C and increasing the precursor–substrate distance, as in the previous experiments at low pressure, SnS was predominant (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">TGA of the samples under O<sub>2</sub>/Ar showed a considerable mass loss at temperatures higher than 550 °C due to the C combustion, and the XRD analysis of the residue showed the complete conversion of SnSe<sub>2</sub> into SnO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a,f</a>). The SnSe<sub>2</sub> content of the samples was calculated from this data to be around 15–30 wt%.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i42" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i42"> Tin Dichalcogenide-HC Composites by FB-CVD</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to scale up the process to the gram scale and further improve the uniformity of the coatings, we developed a FB-CVD strategy. We designed a quartz reactor as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,c, consisting of a bulb-shaped reaction zone and a disk of sintered glass as the reactor bed, functioning as a distributor, where the HC powders were loaded. The main nitrogen flow passed through a quartz tube from the bottom of the reactor through the distributor, applying pressure to the HC powders. The reactor was placed in a vertical tubular furnace so that the whole bulb would be within the hot zone of the furnace. Once stabilized at the reaction temperature and under nitrogen flow, the precursor, as a solution in toluene, was dropwise injected with a syringe via a long needle, at 2 cm from the top of the distributor. A syringe pump was used to control the injection rate.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the first instance, we focused on the deposition of SnSe<sub>2</sub> from [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] on HC powders in a FB-CVD set up at different temperatures and flows. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> shows samples obtained after 40 min deposition at 430 °C, consisting of SnSe<sub>2</sub> flakes on the surface of HC, as observed from SEM analysis. EDX acquired at different locations showed consistently a Sn:Se ratio close to 1:2. EDX acquired at the spot indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c (yellow cross) showed the following composition: Se L 5.01 at. %, Sn L 2.81 at. %, C K 92.18 at. %. The composition was confirmed by XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a), showing sharp 2H-SnSe<sub>2</sub> peaks. A minor component of SnSe was observed by the appearance of a peak at 37.9°. In the preliminary conditions explored, we consistently observed a significant loss of material by entrainment. Compared to plug flow CVD, the powders are very mobile in the fluidized bed and are not completely confined to the reaction zone. A finer component of the powders can move to the higher part of the reactor, where lower temperatures are experienced so that some deposition can take place in this colder zone of the furnace. The percentage of SnSe<sub>2</sub> was determined by TGA under an atmosphere of Ar and O<sub>2</sub> showing a mass loss around 600 °C. XRD of the TGA residue confirmed a complete conversion to SnO<sub>2</sub> under these conditions and the sample composition was calculated to be in the order of 7 wt% SnSe<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Powder XRD by capillary transmission of an HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> sample obtained by FB-CVD at 430 °C and SEM micrographs obtained at lower (b) and higher (c) magnification.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">These results demonstrated that the high temperature injection of a precursor solution in a fluidized powder bed is a viable route for the synthesis of metal-chalcogenide-coated carbon composite materials. Nevertheless, the fluidization was not stable but bubbling and turbulent, leading to substantial entrainment. This behavior was expected for the HC, which consisted of fine powders with an average 10 μm grain size and low density, hence belonging to the Geldart group C classification. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref29" aria-label="reference 27 and 29">(27,29)</a> For such particles, cohesive van der Waals forces prevail, causing agglomerates to form in situ and substantially broaden the actual particle size distribution. Bigger agglomerates require higher gas velocities to be fluidized, but at such higher gas velocities, smaller particles can escape the reactor. The coexistence of particles of different sizes thus makes smooth fluidization difficult.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i43" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i43"> CFD Study</h3><div class="NLM_p">A CFD evaluation was conducted to evaluate the gaseous and solid flow dynamics within the proposed reactor. For the simulation, HC powders were represented as spherical particles of 10 μm diameter with a density of 1.52 g cm<sup>–3</sup>. Simulations were run at gas velocities of 0.1, 0.15, and 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup> to identify the range in which the powders are fluidized. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> (left panels) shows a comparative study of the carbon volume fraction at different gas velocities of inlet N<sub>2</sub> in the first seconds, i.e., after 0.5 and 1.0 s for nitrogen inlet velocities 0.1 and 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup> along the central <i>XY</i>-plane of the bulb reactor. For both inlet velocities, the particle bed resides in the lower region of the bed with denser packing observed toward the annular region of the reactor and a dilute core. This is expected given that these Geldart C particles have high frictional stresses due to high interparticle forces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The cohesive nature of such small particles demonstrates the agglomeration of particles and the creation of a gas-channel through the center of the reactor. For both inlet velocities, the maximum volume fraction is higher at 0.5 s than at 1.0 s, indicating that the bed had yet to reach fluidized state. This is reinforced with a slightly higher bed height observed at 1.0 s compared with 0.5 s. Increasing the inlet velocity to 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup> further aerates the particles, promoting a more dilute flow of the particles which can observed at both 0.5 and 1.0 s of fluidization time.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. CFD comparison study of the volume fraction (left panels) and of the <i>y</i>-velocity (right panel) of carbon for different nitrogen inlet velocities along the central plane of the reactor after 0.5 and 1.0 s.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <i>y</i>-velocity directional component of the carbon across the central <i>XY</i>-plane is presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> (right panels) and shows more clearly the extent of carbon distribution within the bulb reactor for the different nitrogen inlet velocities, 1.0 and 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup>. Given that this is the velocity specifically captured in the <i>y</i>-direction, it is clear that there are particles upward, i.e., <i>v</i><sub>p</sub> &gt; 0 m s<sup>–1</sup>, and particles traveling back toward the inlet, i.e., <i>v</i><sub>p</sub> &lt; 0 m s<sup>–1</sup>. Cross-referencing against <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> the <i>y</i>-velocity distributions suggests that the particles travel further into the freeboard of the reactor than the volume fraction contours suggest.</div><div class="NLM_p">Initial observations show a faster upward velocity near the gas inlet. A significant particle presence is observed above the central part of the bed, which lies above the central gas channel. Closer inspection of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> suggests that these particles are traveling downward and, moreover, reducing in occurrence as the fluidization time increases. This is very likely attributed to the expulsion of particles that occurs once the gas-channel breaks through the bed. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> shows an expansion of the negative and positive <i>y</i>-velocities in the run up to the 0.5 s point, specifically at 0.2 0.3, and 0.4 s for a nitrogen inlet velocity of 0.1 m s<sup>–1</sup>. At 0.2 s, the particles are traveling upward with a greater magnitude than those descending. There is a clear circulation to the flow in the annular of the reactor with a central upward flow of particles driven by the gas traveling through the gas channel. By 0.3 s, there is a great downward velocity as many of the particles return to the bed. The maximum upward velocity is also lower, which is expected as the flow responds to the freeboard and the divergent geometry of the reactor. These trends continue for the downward and upward flow at 0.4 s and the magnitudes at 0.5 s in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> for 0.1 m s<sup>–1</sup>, although the particles exhibiting a positive <i>y</i>-velocities at 0.4 s do not travel as high into the freeboard, whereas those that are in the freeboard are descending.</div><div class="NLM_p">A further observation is that at 0.3 and 0.4 s in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, and at 0.5 s in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, there is an increasing presence of particles with a positive <i>y</i>-velocity nearer the annular near the walls. This will likely be due to walls offering a more favorable means for the gas to traverse around the denser particle regions, which will have the higher interparticle forces. It was previously noted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> at 1.0 s that for both the 0.1 and 0.2 m s<sup>–1</sup> inlet velocities the particle bed appeared more dilute and aerated, and this is further observed with the <i>y</i>-velocity distributions in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> with a slightly higher bed expansion and slightly more circulatory flows.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Positive and negative velocities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S9 and S10</a>) furthermore show that there is entrainment of particles early in the process, as a response to the gas breaking through the bed and the gas channeling into the freeboard with a great inlet velocity. After 0.4 s, it is clear that slower particles have returned to the base of the reactor and fluidization continues. The close up in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a> better shows the extent of mixing within the particle bed. Circulatory flow is evident, with the flow driven from faster flow at the walls and particles rolling back into the core of the reactor. Downward particles come from the freeboard, where particles have dropped back down to the bed, while the central core provides a faster stream of particle flow in response to the fast inlet velocity through the center of the bed.</div></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i44" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i44"> Optimal FB-CVD Reactor Design for the Synthesis of Tin Dichalcogenide-HC Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p">This study confirmed that at the gas velocities of 0.15 m s<sup>–1</sup> used in our method, there is a significant entrainment of carbon particles. It also suggests that gas velocities as low as 0.1 m s<sup>–1</sup> may allow a more significant confinement of the particles in the reactor’s bed and a sufficient bed expansion to allow FB-CVD. For these reasons, we focused on lower gas velocities of the inlet N<sub>2</sub> but aimed at improving the fluidization by modifying the reactor geometry and by aiding powder mixing. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, a thin quartz tube with 1 mm tip diameter was introduced, allowing addition of a secondary inlet of nitrogen flowing directly on the reactor bed, to improve powder mixing and to avoid the formation of agglomerates. The precursor was also injected through this thin tube and transported to the reactor bed. A (removable) shield and a sintered quartz filter were added to the top of the main outlet to better retain the powders within the reaction zone. The main and secondary nitrogen flows were regulated to 0.5 L/min. These measures allowed retention of 30 to 50% of the HC powders.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Sketch of the modified FB-CVD reactor; (b) XRD; lower (c) and higher (d) magnification SEM micrographs and of a HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> sample obtained by FB-CVD at 430 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Samples obtained by FB-CVD with the modified reactor from the [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] precursor on 1 g of HC, at 430 °C, are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b–d. XRD shows the clear presence of SnSe<sub>2</sub> and a smaller contribution from SnSe (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). SEM at lower magnification evidence the presence of high electron contrast areas uniformly distributed on the powder surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). At higher SEM magnification, it is possible to distinguish SnSe<sub>2</sub> flakes, with high electron contrast and a typical planar structure, emerging from the surface of HC, showing the formation of a direct HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> interface. A 13 wt% loading of SnSe<sub>2</sub> was measured by TGA in Ar/O<sub>2</sub> with the residue consisting of SnO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> samples obtained by FB-CVD were acquired and compared to those of pure HC. HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> showed a higher specific surface area of 12 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> compared to 2.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> of untreated HC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). The increased surface area of the composites could be attributed to their higher surface roughness, which is also evident by observing the composites’ morphology at SEM. All samples showed a broad pore size distribution (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>), which is dominated by the particles packing rather than an intrinsic porosity.</div></div><div id="sec3_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i45" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i45"> Synthesis of SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub>–SnSe<sub>2</sub> layers on HC</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to make layers of the different materials, we performed two separate injections of the two precursors. A first dropwise injection of the [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] precursor was performed at 350 °C, and after 30 min, the temperature was raised to 430 °C, at which the [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] precursor solution was injected dropwise. XRD showed the prevalence of SnSe<sub>2</sub> and a minor contribution of SnSe, while the presence of either SnS<sub>2</sub> or SnS was not evident. Nevertheless, the presence of Sn, Se, and S species was confirmed by SEM/EDS, with a ratio of metal to chalcogen close to 1:2, while the absence of Cl-containing species ruled out the presence of unreacted precursors (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,c). TGA showed a residual mass comparable to or higher than the one observed for the HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7d</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/medium/ao4c00297_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Capillary transmission XRD, (b) SEM, (c) EDS, and (d) elemental mapping of HC-SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub>–SnSe<sub>2</sub> synthesized by FB-CVD by multiple injections. (e, f) High resolution SE micrographs of a single particle, also acquired with a backscattering detector (f), and (g) corresponding elemental mapping.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/asset/images/large/ao4c00297_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.4c00297&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the HC-SnS<sub>x</sub>–SnSe<sub>2</sub> samples showed a specific surface area of 5.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> t, higher than untreated HC but lower than single deposit HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> composites (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). A possible explanation is that the second material deposits by filling the gaps in the original material and could partially account for the lower crystallinity. The SEM study also shows samples with slightly different morphology and surface roughness (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,e,f).</div><div class="NLM_p last">Elemental mapping at low magnification showed a clear presence of Sn, Se, and S in the same areas with similar intensity, indicating that the species are deposited uniformly throughout the sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d). The absence of clear SnS<sub>2</sub> or SnS peaks in the XRD analysis could possibly be due to a low crystallinity. It should be highlighted that SnS<sub>2</sub> coatings previously deposited in different crystalline phases, e.g., 2H-bulk, 4H-bulk, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and cubic <i>Fd</i>3̅<i>m</i> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), were expected to show a predominant peak at 14° due to the 001 reflection, which overlaps with the 001 reflection of SnSe<sub>2</sub> and could therefore be masked by a larger amount of that phase. An insight at higher magnification is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e–g, displaying thin platelets protruding out of a single carbon particle, which represents a tantalizingly promising morphology for application as a battery anode active material. In light of the potential applications in electrochemistry, the high surface area reduces the proportion of electrochemically inactive material found in the center of large particles and affords a high rate capability of charge/discharge, simultaneously providing the requisite space for the metal dichalcogenide crystal lattice to expand/contract upon charge/discharge cycling of the battery, minimizing electrode cracking, and extending useful battery lifetime. This material is advantageously anchored to a high conductivity carbon core, which could facilitate electronic transport out to the current collector foil and into the electronic circuit beyond. This is more evident in the backscattering EM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f, where the high contrast platelets can be attributed to high electron scattering deposited species. Interestingly, the corresponding elemental mapping shows that SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub> and SnSe<sub>2</sub> were deposited in the same areas, with the possible formation of a layered structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). This finding is of particular importance for novel next generation battery materials, indicating that this method can be used to fabricate multilayered composites. By choosing appropriate combinations of materials, it would be possible to engineer “metamaterials” and tune the heterolattice coatings properties, such as band-edge staggering at the interface of the inorganic layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> In particular, combination of SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnSe<sub>2</sub> layers on HC is a promising anode material Na-ion batteries, with the with SnS<sub>2</sub>-SnSe<sub>2</sub> layered systems allowing for high energy density on conductive HC. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i46">Conclusions and Outlook</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Our findings show that CVD is a viable route for the synthesis of powder composites based on carbon, with controlled composition and a layered structure of tin dichalcogenides, which are extremely promising candidates for applications in batteries and electrocatalysis owing to control of morphology and the opportunity for bandgap engineering. Plug flow-CVD offered a solution to deposit metal dichalcogenides on HC powders with high deposition yield on small amounts of powders, and the process was scaled up to few hundred milligrams of material by FB-CVD. An optimized reactor configuration allowed us to minimize the natural entrainment of fine low-density powders (belonging to the Geldart C classification), with the turbulent fluidization allowing for good mixing and material uniformity. This method holds promise for generalization of the uniform CVD coating of fine powders of more different materials from either solid and liquid precursors. We observed that the retention of particles lies not only with hydrodynamics operating conditions such as velocities but also with the reactor design, with the possibility to further improve the powder retention and aim at a high nanocomposite yield. Finally, by alternating the injection of different precursors, we achieved the deposition of two different materials on the HC powders, paving the way to the fabrication on metamaterials with controlled properties such as band edge staggering and tuned energy density for applications as energy materials with important implications for the design and synthesis of novel next generation battery active materials and metamaterials.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i47">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline"><sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] and [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] precursors. XRD and SEM of SnSe<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> thin films on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> substrates obtained by LP-CVD from single-source precursors; SEM and EDX spectra of tin dichalcogenide thin films on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> tiles obtained by LP-CVD from single-source precursors; powder XRD and SEM of HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> composites obtained by plug flow-CVD at different temperatures; TGA of composites synthesized by plug-flow CVD and FB-CVD in different conditions and powder XRD of the residue; CFD close up of the specific directional <i>y</i>-velocities along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.1 m/s inlet velocity at different times; positive and negative <i>y</i>-velocities within the bulb along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.4 m/s inlet velocity at varying fluidization times and comparison with the positive only <i>y</i>-velocities; close up of the positive and negative <i>y</i>-velocities along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.4 m/s inlet velocity at 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 s BET analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf">ao4c00297_si_001.pdf (1.6 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.4c00297" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

Soc.y Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>42</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3088</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3113</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2cs35307e</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D42%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D3088-3113%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.y%2BRev.%26author%3DH.%2BWang%26author%3DH.%2BDai%26title%3DStrongly%2Bcoupled%2Binorganic%25E2%2580%2593nano-carbon%2Bhybrid%2Bmaterials%2Bfor%2Benergy%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc2cs35307e&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc2cs35307e&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Strongly coupled inorganic-nano-carbon hybrid materials for energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Hailiang; Dai, Hongjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">42</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3088-3113</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The global shift of energy prodn. from fossil fuels to renewable energy sources requires more efficient and reliable electrochem. energy storage devices.  In particular, the development of elec. or hydrogen powered vehicles calls for much-higher-performance batteries, supercapacitors and fuel cells than are currently available.  In this review, we present an approach to synthesize electrochem. energy storage materials to form strongly coupled hybrids (SC-hybrids) of inorg. nanomaterials and novel graphitic nano-carbon materials such as carbon nanotubes and graphene, through nucleation and growth of nanoparticles at the functional groups of oxidized graphitic nano-carbon.  We show that the inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials represent a new approach to synthesize electrode materials with higher electrochem. performance than traditional counterparts made by simple phys. mixts. of electrochem. active inorg. particles and conducting carbon materials.  The inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials are novel due to possible chem. bonding between inorg. nanoparticles and oxidized carbon, affording enhanced charge transport and increased rate capability of electrochem. materials without sacrificing specific capacity.  Nano-carbon with various degrees of oxidn. provides a novel substrate for nanoparticle nucleation and growth.  The interactions between inorg. precursors and oxidized-carbon substrates provide a degree of control over the morphol., size and structure of the resulting inorg. nanoparticles.  This paper reviews the recent development of inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials for electrochem. energy storage and conversion, including the prepn. and functionalization of graphene sheets and carbon nanotubes to impart oxygen contg. groups and defects, and methods of synthesis of nanoparticles of various morphologies on oxidized graphene and carbon nanotubes.  We then review the applications of the SC-hybrid materials for high performance lithium ion batteries, rechargeable Li-S and Li-O2 batteries, supercapacitors and ultrafast Ni-Fe batteries, and new electrocatalysts for oxygen redn., oxygen evolution and hydrogen evolution reactions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.pmatsci.2020.100717"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ciucci, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-based electrocatalysts for sustainable energy applications</span>. <i>Prog. Mater. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>116</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">100717</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.pmatsci.2020.100717</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D116%26publication_year%3D2021%26journal%3DProg.%2BMater.%2BSci.%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DH.%2BKong%26author%3DJ.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BHao%26author%3DZ.%2BShao%26author%3DF.%2BCiucci%26title%3DCarbon-based%2Belectrocatalysts%2Bfor%2Bsustainable%2Benergy%2Bapplications%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.pmatsci.2020.100717&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.pmatsci.2020.100717&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-based electrocatalysts for sustainable energy applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jian; Kong, Hui; Zhang, Jinying; Hao, Yong; Shao, Zongping; Ciucci, Francesco</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Progress in Materials Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">116</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">100717</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRMSAQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0079-6425</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon-based materials have multiple advantages including abundant sources, tunable mol. structures, high electronic cond., and environmental compatibility.  Rapidly growing research interests are focused on carbon materials as electrocatalysts for oxygen redn. reaction (ORR), oxygen evolution reaction (OER), and hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), which are crit. for sustainable energy technologies including fuel cells, metal-air batteries, and water electrolyzers.  A no. of papers in this area have been published.  There is no systematic review yet covering all general methods to boost carbon-based electrocatalysts for ORR/OER/HER, and reporting their most recent progress.  To eliminate the gap, this contribution gives a timely and comprehensive review of such a rapidly growing field of great significance.  Brief introduction regarding the fundamentals of carbon-based ORR/OER/HER electrocatalysts and proceeds to summarize various synthesis methods of state-of-the-art carbon materials.  Most popular strategies to optimize electrocatalytic properties of carbons are thoroughly reviewed.  In addn. to metal-free carbons, advances regarding hybridized and self-supported carbon electrocatalysts are discussed.  Comprehensive review not only conveys most recent research progress, but also provides essential ideas and methods that guide design of high-performance carbon-based electrocatalysts, contributing to their large-scale adoption in sustainable energy applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500486u"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Munnik, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">de Jongh, P. E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">de Jong, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Photoelectrocatalysis (PEC) technol. has attracted broad interest due to its great application potential in pollutant removal and energy prodn.  However, the high recombination of photogenerated electron-hole pairs, low utilization efficiency of solar energy, and limited reaction area are still the main obstacles that limit the practical applications of PEC.  Carbon-based materials with unique and excellent properties are widely coupled with semiconductors to prep. composite photoelectrocatalysts to address the above problems.  Carbon-based materials can serve as supporting materials, conductive substrates, electron transfer media, photosensitizers, and stabilizers to greatly improve the photoelectrocatalytic performance of semiconductors.  Meanwhile, carbon-based materials can exert semiconductor properties to generate electron-hole pairs or construct heterojunctions with semiconductors to efficiently promote charge sepn.  In this review, a comprehensive summary is given to introduce synthetic methods and improvement strategies of carbon-based material/semiconductor composite photoelectrocatalysts, as well as their latest applications in the removal of org. pollutants.  Esp., the roles of carbon-based materials in the PEC process are highlighted.  Finally, current challenges and prospects are put forward.  We do hope that this article can outline the recent advances and provide new insights for the future development of carbon-based semiconductor photoelectrocatalysts applied in water treatment.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4CS00258J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhag, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Chemical vapour deposition of group-VIB metal dichalcogenide monolayers: engineered substrates from amorphous to single crystalline</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">As structural analogs of graphene but with a sizeable band gap, monolayers of group-VIB transition metal dichalcogenides (MX2, M = Mo, W; X = S, Se, Te, etc.) have emerged as the ideal two dimensional prototype for exploring fundamental issues in physics such as valley polarization, and for engineering a wide range of nanoelectronic, optoelectronic and photocatalytic applications.  Recently, chem. vapor deposition (CVD) was introduced as a more efficient prepn. method than traditional chem. or phys. exfoliation options, and has allowed for the successful synthesis of large-area MX2 monolayers possessing a large domain size, high thickness uniformity and continuity, and satisfactory crystal quality.  This tutorial review therefore focuses on introducing the more recent advances in the CVD growth of MX2 (MoS2, WS2, MoS2(1-x)Se2xetc.) monolayers via the sulphurization/decompn. of pre-deposited metal-based precursors, or the one-step reaction and deposition of gaseous metal and chalcogen feedstocks.  Differences in growth behavior caused by commonly used amorphous SiO2/Si, and newly adopted insulating single crystal substrates such as sapphire, mica and SrTiO3, are also comparatively presented.  Also discussed are the essential parameters that influence the growth of MX2, such as the temp., the source-substrate distance and the compn. of the carrier gas (Ar/H2).     </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin two-dimensional nanosheets of layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) are fundamentally and technologically intriguing.  In contrast to the graphene sheet, they are chemically versatile.  Mono- or few-layered TMDs - obtained either through exfoliation of bulk materials or bottom-up syntheses - are direct-gap semiconductors whose bandgap energy, as well as carrier type (n- or p-type), varies between compounds depending on their composition, structure and dimensionality.  In this Review, we describe how the tunable electronic structure of TMDs makes them attractive for a variety of applications.  They have been investigated as chemically active electrocatalysts for hydrogen evolution and hydrosulfurization, as well as electrically active materials in opto-electronics.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A facile and fruitful CVD method is reported for the first time, to synthesize high-quality hexagonal SnS2 nanosheets on carbon cloth via in situ sulfurization of SnO2.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal chalcogenide thin films have a wide variety of applications and potential uses.  Tin(II) sulfide is one such material which presents a significant challenge with the need for high quality SnS, free of oxide materials (e.g., SnO2) and higher tin sulfides (e.g., Sn2S3 and SnS2).  This problem is compounded further when the target material exhibits a no. of polymorphic forms with different optoelectronic properties.  Unlike conventional chem. vapor deposition (CVD) and at. layer deposition (ALD), which rely heavily on having precursors that are volatile, stable, and reactive, the use of aerosol assisted CVD (AA-CVD) negates the need for volatile precursors.  We report here, for the first time, the novel and structurally characterized single source precursor (1), (dimethylamido)(N-phenyl-N',N'-dimethylthiouriate)tin(II) dimer, and its application in the deposition, by AA-CVD, of phase-pure films of SnS.  A mechanism for the oxidatively controlled formation of SnS from precursor 1 is also reported.  Significantly, thermal control of the deposition process allows for the unprecedented selective and exclusive formation of either orthorhombic-SnS (α-SnS) or zinc blende-SnS (ZB-SnS) polymorphs.  Thin films of α-SnS or ZB-SnS have been deposited onto Mo, fluorine doped tin oxide (FTO), Si, and glass substrates at the optimized deposition temps. of 375 and 300 °C, resp.  The densely packed polycryst. thin films have been characterized by X-ray diffraction, SEM, at. force microscopy, Raman spectroscopy, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy, and XPS anal.  These data confirmed the phase purity of the SnS formed.  Optical anal. of the α-SnS and ZB-SnS films shows distinctly different optical properties with direct band gaps of 1.34 and 1.78 eV, resp.  Furthermore, photoelectrochem. and external quantum efficiency (EQE) measurements were undertaken to assess the optoelectronic properties of the deposited samples.  We also report for the first time the ambipolar properties of the ZB-SnS phase.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1063/1.2894903"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wamwangi, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Woda, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wuttig, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bensch, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Investigation of SnSe, SnSe<sub>2</sub>, and Sn<sub>2</sub>Se<sub>3</sub> alloys for phase change memory applications</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultralow thermal conductivity and high thermoelectric figure of merit in SnSe crystals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Li-Dong; Lo, Shih-Han; Zhang, Yongsheng; Sun, Hui; Tan, Gangjian; Uher, Ctirad; Wolverton, C.; Dravid, Vinayak P.; Kanatzidis, Mercouri G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature (London, United Kingdom)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The thermoelec. effect enables direct and reversible conversion between thermal and elec. energy, and provides a viable route for power generation from waste heat.  The efficiency of thermoelec. materials is dictated by the dimensionless figure of merit, ZT (where Z is the figure of merit and T is abs. temp.), which governs the Carnot efficiency for heat conversion.  Enhancements above the generally high threshold value of 2.5 have important implications for com. deployment, esp. for compds. free of Pb and Te.  Here we report an unprecedented ZT of 2.6 ± 0.3 at 923 K, realized in SnSe single crystals measured along the b axis of the room-temp. orthorhombic unit cell.  This material also shows a high ZT of 2.3 ± 0.3 along the c axis but a significantly reduced ZT of 0.8 ± 0.2 along the a axis.  We attribute the remarkably high ZT along the b axis to the intrinsically ultralow lattice thermal cond. in SnSe.  The layered structure of SnSe derives from a distorted rock-salt structure, and features anomalously high Grueneisen parameters, which reflect the anharmonic and anisotropic bonding.  We attribute the exceptionally low lattice thermal cond. (0.23 ± 0.03 W m-1 K-1 at 973 K) in SnSe to the anharmonicity.  These findings highlight alternative strategies to nanostructuring for achieving high thermoelec. performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C7RA00373K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved thermoelectric properties of SnS synthesized by chemical precipitation</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">16795</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two-dimensional (2D) nanomaterials have been attracting intensive interest due to their unique phys. and optoelectronic properties.  Van der Waals nanolayered heterostructures based on different 2D nanomaterials provide a state-of-the-art platform for investigating the phys. phenomena and have extended the applications of 2D nanomaterials, leading to an era of next-generation high-performance electronic and optoelectronic technol.  Herein, vertical 2D SnS/SnS2 p-n and SnSe2/SnS2 n-n nanolayered heterostructures are constructed by exfoliating individual materials and stacking them artificially.  The two types of back-gate field-effect phototransistors show clear rectifying behaviors with the existence of band offset and excellent optoelectronic performances.  The SnS/SnS2 p-n nanolayered heterostructure shows an ambipolar behavior with a hole mobility of 6.96 cm2·V-1·s-1 and electron mobility of 0.68 cm2·V-1·s-1, while the SnSe2/SnS2 n-n nanolayered heterostructure shows an intense n-type behavior with electron mobility of 3.78 cm2·V-1·s-1 and a high on/off ratio of over 105.  Interestingly, due to the band alignments and built-in potentials in the two types of nanolayered heterostructures, the phototransistors exhibit enhanced responsive performances to illuminations of three-primary-color lasers (λ = 405, 532, and 650 nm) compared to the individual components.  The SnS/SnS2 phototransistor shows a high photoresponsivity of 3567 A W-1, a high detectivity of 3.64 x 1013 Jones, and a fast response time of 0.8 ms.  The SnSe2/SnS2 phototransistor also exhibits a high photoresponsivity of 1793 A W-1 and a fast response time of 15.4 ms.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1039/C7CP03748A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shafique, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Samad, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shin, Y.-H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultra low lattice thermal conductivity and high carrier mobility of monolayer SnS<sub>2</sub> and SnSe<sub>2</sub>: a first principle study</span>. <i>Phys.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultra low lattice thermal conductivity and high carrier mobility of monolayer SnS2 and SnSe2: a first principles study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shafique, Aamir; Samad, Abdus; Shin, Young-Han</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Using d. functional theory, the authors systematically investigate the lattice thermal cond. and carrier mobility of monolayer SnX2 (X = S, Se).  The room-temp. ultra low lattice thermal conductivities found in monolayer SnS2 (6.41 W m-1 K-1) and SnSe2 (3.82 W m-1 K-1) are attributed to the low phonon velocity, low Debye temp., weak bonding interactions, and strong anharmonicity in monolayer SnX2.  The predicted values of lattice thermal cond. are lower than those of other 2-dimensional materials such as stanene, phosphorene, monolayer MoS2, and bulk SnX2.  High phonon-limited carrier mobilities are obtained for the monolayer SnX2.  For example, the electron mobility of monolayer SnS2 is 756.60 cm2 V-1 s-1 and the hole mobility is 187.44 cm2 V-1 s-1.  The electron mobility of these monolayers is higher than their hole mobility due to the low effective mass of electrons and low deformation consts., which makes them n-type materials.  Due to their ultra low lattice thermal conductivities coupled with high carrier mobilities, monolayer SnX2 materials may be promising materials for thermoelec. applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201809847"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, K.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Mini Review on Transition Metal Chalcogenides for Electrocatalytic Water Splitting: Bridging Material Design and Practical Application</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongjie; Guo, Yajie; Liu, Yanrong; Wei, Zhilei; Wang, Ke; Shi, Zhongqi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Fuels</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  H is believed to be one of the essential clean secondary energy sources in the energy structure revolution of both industry and daily life.  Driven by renewable electricity such as solar and wind power, H2O electrolysis for H prodn. is deemed as one of the main processes of green H prodn. in the future by both academia and industry.  Transition metal chalcogenides (TMCs) are promising candidates to replace noble metals as earth-abundant electrocatalysts for H2O splitting.  However, it remains challenging to further improve the electrocatalytic activity and long-term stability of TMCs, esp. in a practical H2O electrolyzer.  This Review summarizes the recent advances and the strategies of optimizing the electrocatalytic activities of TMCs toward H2O splitting as well as the latest studies on the surface reconstructions of TMCs during H2O electrolysis.  The performances of TMCs in practical electrocatalytic H2O splitting cells are particularly discussed.  Finally, a concluding remark and perspective is provided, and the authors hope to inspire future works in this area, narrowing the gap between material design and practical application.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Giuffredi, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Asset, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Atanassov, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Di Fonzo, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition metal chalcogenides as a versatile and tunable platform for catalytic CO<sub>2</sub> and N<sub>2</sub> electroreduction</span>. <i>ACS Mater.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Group VI transition metal chalcogenides are the subject of increasing research interest for various electrochem. applications such as low-temp. water electrolysis, batteries, and supercapacitors due to their high activity, chem. stability, and the strong correlation between structure and electrochem. properties.  Particularly appealing is their utilization as electrocatalysts for the synthesis of energy vectors and value-added chems. such as C-based chems. from the CO2 redn. reaction (CO2R) or ammonia from the nitrogen fixation reaction (NRR).  This review discusses the role of structural and electronic properties of transition metal chalcogenides in enhancing selectivity and activity toward these two key redn. reactions.  First, we discuss the morphol. and electronic structure of these compds., outlining design strategies to control and fine-tune them.  Then, we discuss the role of the active sites and the strategies developed to enhance the activity of transition metal chalcogenide-based catalysts in the framework of CO2R and NRR against the parasitic hydrogen evolution reaction (HER); leveraging on the design rules applied for HER applications, we discuss their future perspective for the applications in CO2R and NRR.  For these two reactions, we comprehensively review recent progress in unveiling reaction mechanisms at different sites and the most effective strategies for fabricating catalysts that, by exploiting the structural and electronic peculiarities of transition metal chalcogenides, can outperform many metallic compds.  Transition metal chalcogenides outperform state-of-the-art catalysts for CO2 to CO redn. in ionic liqs. due to the favorable CO2 adsorption on the metal edge sites, whereas the basal sites, due to their conformation, represent an appealing design space for redn. of CO2 to complex carbon products.  For the NRR instead, the resemblance of transition metal chalcogenides to the active centers of nitrogenase enzymes represents a powerful nature-mimicking approach for the design of catalysts with enhanced performance, although strategies to hinder the HER must be integrated in the catalytic architecture.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1039/D2TA09741A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review on the advancement of SnS<sub>2</sub> in photocatalysis</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Spontaneous full photocatalytic water splitting into hydrogen and oxygen under visible light irradn. without the need for sacrificial agents is a challenging task, because suitable band gaps, low overpotentials for both half-reactions and spatially-sepd. catalytic sites should be fulfilled simultaneously in a photocatalytic system.  Here, we propose a promising strategy to achieve this goal by constructing van der Waals (vdW) heterostructures of two-dimensional (2D) materials.  Using first-principles calcns., we predict two promising photocatalysts, MoSe2/SnSe2 and WSe2/SnSe2 heterostructures, with the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) and oxygen evolution reaction (OER) taking place sep. on the MoSe2 (WSe2) and SnSe2 layers.  More excitingly, the Se-vacancy of the MoSe2 (WSe2) monolayer effectively lowers the HER overpotential, making the catalytic reactions occur spontaneously under the potentials solely provided by the photo-generated electrons and holes in pure water.  The unique band alignment of these hetero-structured photocatalysts leads to high solar-to-hydrogen (STH) energy conversion efficiencies up to 10.5%, which is quite promising for com. applications.  This work opens up an avenue for the design of highly-efficient photocatalysts for full water splitting.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/D3TA00940H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Price, C. The behavior of powders is characterized by 4 groups according to d. difference (ρs-ρf) and mean size.  Group A powders show dense-phase expansion after min. fluidization and prior to the commencement of bubbling.  Group B powders bubble at the min. fluidization velocity.  Group C is difficult to fluidize, while group D includes large size and/or d. and spouts readily.  A criterion distinguishing between groups A and B is given and agrees well with published data; it also predicts that a change in pressure and/or gas viscosity may cause a change in the behavior of particles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1007/s11051-012-0737-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van
Ommen, J. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Valverde, J. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pfeffer, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluidization of nanopowders: a review</span>. <i>J. Nanopart. Res.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>14</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">737</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluidization of nanopowders: a review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">van Ommen, J. Ruud; Valverde, Jose Manuel; Pfeffer, Robert</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Nanoparticle Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">737/1-737/29</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JNARFA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1388-0764</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Nanoparticles (NPs) are applied in a wide range of processes, and their use continues to increase.  Fluidization is one of the best techniques available to disperse and process NPs.  NPs cannot be fluidized individually; they fluidize as very porous agglomerates.  The objective of this article is to review the developments in nanopowder fluidization.  Often, it is needed to apply an assistance method, such as vibration or microjets, to obtain proper fluidization.  These methods can greatly improve the fluidization characteristics, strongly increase the bed expansion, and lead to a better mixing of the bed material.  Several approaches have been applied to model the behavior of fluidized nanopowders.  The av. size of fluidized NP agglomerates can be estd. using a force balance or by a modified Richardson and Zaki equation.  Some first attempts have been made to apply computational fluid dynamics.  Fluidization can also be used to provide individual NPs with a thin coating of another material and to mix two different species of nanopowder.  The application of nanopowder fluidization in practice is still limited, but a wide range of potential applications is foreseen.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.powtec.2020.12.011"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A review on fluidization of Geldart Group C powders through nanoparticle modulation</span>. <i>Powder Technology</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>381</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">698</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A review on fluidization of Geldart Group C powders through nanoparticle modulation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Yandaizi; Zhu, Jesse</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Powder Technology</span>
