        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Geldart Group C powders with large sp. surface area have been applied in a variety of processes.  Gas-solid fluidization is a popular technique for processing these particles.  However, most Group C particles are not fluidizable due to strong interparticle forces.  In recent decades, one effective technique assisting their fluidization has been the addn. of small amts. of nanoparticles to reduce their interparticle forces.  This paper reviews both theor. and exptl. studies on the gas fluidization of Group C particles, including nanoparticle modulation techniques, surface morphol., and modeling of interparticle forces.  More importantly, the fluidization of these coated Group C particles (the so-called Group C+ particles) have been comprehensively discussed.  New parameters were proposed to analyze the effects of different forces on their fluidization.  The performance of fluidized bed reactors using Group C+ catalysts is also reviewed, albeit more works are still needed before the extended applications in chem. engineering.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1002/cvde.200606533"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, W</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagen, V</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Heel, A</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kasper, G</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Patil, U</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Devi, A</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muhler, M</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Synthesis of ZrO<sub>2</sub>/SiO<sub>2</sub> Nanocomposites by the Two-Step CVD of a Volatile Halogen-Free Zr Alkoxide in a Fluidized-Bed Reactor</span>. <i>Chem. In this process, the treated object is suspended within the FB reactor, which contains the treating agent.  The latter is a mixt. of powders of the donor of the coating-forming element (FexAly), and of a filler material (Al2O3).  As an activator of the process, a halide compd. is used (NH4Cl).         </div><div class="casAbstract">This work reports exptl. results on the heat transfer between a fluidized bed of fine particles and a submerged surface.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly selective chemical vapor deposition of tin diselenide thin films onto patterned substrates via single source diselenoether precursors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">de Groot, C. H.; Gurnani, Chitra; Hector, Andrew L.; Huang, Ruomeng; Jura, Marek; Levason, William; Reid, Gillian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">24</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4442-4449</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The distorted octahedral complexes [SnCl4{nBuSe(CH2)nSenBu}] (n = 2 or 3), (1) and (2), obtained from reaction of SnCl4 with the neutral bidentate ligands and characterized by IR/Raman and multinuclear (1H, 77Se{1H} and 119Sn) NMR spectroscopy and X-ray crystallog., serve as very effective single source precursors for low pressure chem. vapor deposition (LPCVD) of microcryst., single phase tin diselenide films onto SiO2, Si and TiN substrates.  SEM (SEM) and Atomic Force Microscopy (AFM) imaging show hexagonal plate crystallites which grow perpendicular to the substrate surface in the thicker films, but align mostly parallel to the surface when the quantity of reagent is reduced to limit the film thickness.  X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy on the deposited films are consistent with hexagonal SnSe2 (P3 m1; a = b = 3.81 Å; c = 6.13 Å), with strong evidence for preferred orientation of the crystallites in thinner (0.5-2 μm) samples, consistent with crystal plate growth parallel to the substrate surface.  Hall measurements show the deposited SnSe2 is a n-type semiconductor.  The resistivity of the cryst. films is 210 (±10) mΩ cm and carrier d. is 5.0 × 1018 cm-3.  Very highly selective film growth from these reagents onto photolithog. patterned substrates is obsd., with deposition strongly preferred onto the (conducting) TiN surfaces of SiO2/TiN patterned substrates, and onto the SiO2 surfaces of Si/SiO2 patterned substrates.  A correlation between the high selectivity and high contact angle of a water droplet on the substrate surfaces is obsd.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C7DT03848H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gurnani, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hawken, S. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hector, A. B. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin(iv) chalcogenoether complexes as single source precursors for the chemical vapour deposition of SnE<sub>2</sub> and SnE (E = S, Se) thin films</span>. <i>Dalton Trans.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>47</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2628</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7DT03848H</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D47%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D2628%26journal%3DDalton%2BTrans.%26author%3DC.%2BGurnani%26author%3DS.%2BL.%2BHawken%26author%3DA.%2BL.%2BHector%26author%3DR.%2BHuang%26author%3DM.%2BJura%26author%3DW.%2BLevason%26author%3DJ.%2BPerkins%26author%3DG.%2BReid%26author%3DG.%2BB.%2BG.%2BStenning%26title%3DTin%2528iv%2529%2Bchalcogenoether%2Bcomplexes%2Bas%2Bsingle%2Bsource%2Bprecursors%2Bfor%2Bthe%2Bchemical%2Bvapour%2Bdeposition%2Bof%2BSnE2%2Band%2BSnE%2B%2528E%2B%253D%2BS%252C%2BSe%2529%2Bthin%2Bfilms%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC7DT03848H&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC7DT03848H&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin(IV) chalcogenoether complexes as single source precursors for the chemical vapor deposition of SnE2 and SnE (E = S, Se) thin films</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gurnani, Chitra; Hawken, Samantha L.; Hector, Andrew L.; Huang, Ruomeng; Jura, Marek; Levason, William; Perkins, James; Reid, Gillian; Stenning, Gavin B. G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Dalton Transactions</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">47</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2628-2637</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">DTARAF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1477-9226</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The mol. Sn(IV) complexes, [SnCl4{nBuS(CH2)3SnBu}] (2), [SnCl4(nBu2S)2] (3) and [SnCl4(nBu2Se)2] (4) have been prepd. in good yield from reaction of SnCl4 with the appropriate chalcogenoether ligand in anhyd. hexane and, together with the known [SnCl4{nBuSe(CH2)3SenBu}] (1), employed as single source precursors for the low pressure chem. vapor deposition of the corresponding tin dichalcogenide thin films.  At elevated temps. the bidentate ligand precursors, (1) and (2), also form the tin monochalcogenides, SnSe and SnS, resp.  In contrast, (3) gave a mixt. of phases, SnS2, Sn2S3 and SnS and (4) gave SnSe2 only.  The morphologies, elemental compns. and crystal structures of the resulting films have been detd. by SEM, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy, grazing incidence X-ray diffraction and Raman spectroscopy.  Van der Pauw measurements on the SnS2, SnS and SnSe2 films confirm their resistivities to be 2.9(9), 266(3) and 4.4(3) Ω cm, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2006.09.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maeda, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nagai, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goto, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tansho, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hashi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shimizu, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Properties of a novel hard-carbon optimized to large size Li ion secondary battery studied by <sup>7</sup>Li NMR</span>. <i>Journal of Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2006</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>162</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1322</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2006.09.001</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D162%26publication_year%3D2006%26pages%3D1322%26journal%3DJournal%2Bof%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DK.%2BGotoh%26author%3DM.%2BMaeda%26author%3DA.%2BNagai%26author%3DA.%2BGoto%26author%3DM.%2BTansho%26author%3DK.%2BHashi%26author%3DT.%2BShimizu%26author%3DH.%2BIshida%26title%3DProperties%2Bof%2Ba%2Bnovel%2Bhard-carbon%2Boptimized%2Bto%2Blarge%2Bsize%2BLi%2Bion%2Bsecondary%2Bbattery%2Bstudied%2Bby%2B7Li%2BNMR%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2006.09.001&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2006.09.001&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Properties of a novel hard-carbon optimized to large size Li ion secondary battery studied by 7Li NMR</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gotoh, Kazuma; Maeda, Mariko; Nagai, Aisaku; Goto, Atsushi; Tansho, Masataka; Hashi, Kenjiro; Shimizu, Tadashi; Ishida, Hiroyuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2006</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">162</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1322-1328</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The state of Li in a novel hard-C optimized anode for large Li-ion secondary batteries was studied and compared with other hard-C samples by 7Li NMR.  The new C material showed a peak at 85 ppm with a shoulder at 7 ppm at room temp.  In a static NMR spectrum the former shifted to 210 ppm at 180 K.  The latter at room temp. was attributed to Li doped in small particles contained in the sample.  The new C sample showed weaker intensity of cluster-Li signal than the other hard-C samples in NMR, which correspond to a tendency of less const. voltage (CV) capacity in charge-discharge expts.  Smaller CV capacity and initial irreversible capacity, the features of the novel hard-C, correspond to inhibition of Li diffusion into the pores of the C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Syamlal, M.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">O’Brien, T. Fluid Mech.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1987</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>176</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">67</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1017/S0022112087000570</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D176%26publication_year%3D1987%26pages%3D67%26journal%3DJ.%2BFluid%2BMech.%26author%3DP.%2BJohnson%26author%3DR.%2BJackson%26title%3DFrictional-collisional%2Bconstitutive%2Brelations%2Bfor%2Bgranular%2Bmaterials%252C%2Bwith%2Bapplication%2Bto%2Bplane%2Bshearing%26doi%3D10.1017%252FS0022112087000570&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.4c00297&amp;doiOfLink=10.1017%2FS0022112087000570&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Frictional-collisional constitutive relations for granular materials, with application to plane shearing</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Johnson, P. C.; Jackson, R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Fluid Mechanics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1987</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">176</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">67-93</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JFLSA7</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-1120</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">In a granular material, stress is transmitted by forces exerted at points of mutual contact between particles.  When the particles are close together and deformation of the assembly is slow, contacts are sustained for long times, and these forces consist of normal reactions and the assocd. tangential forces due to friction.  When the particles are widely spaced and deformation is rapid, on the other hand, contacts are brief and can be regarded as collisions, during which momentum is transferred.  While constitutive relations are available which model both these situations, in many cases the av. contact times lie between the 2 extremes.  Constitutive relations and boundary conditions are proposed for this intermediate case and to solve the corresponding equations of motion for plane shear of a cohesionless granular material between infinite horizontal plates.  In general, not all the material between the plates participates in shearing, and the solns. for the shearing material are coupled to a yield condition for the nonshearing material to give a complete soln. of the problem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref49" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vasquez, S. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A phase coupled method for solving multiphase problems on unstructured mesh</span>, In:  <i>Proceedings of ASME FEDSM’00: ASME 2000 Fluids Engineering Division Summer Meeting</i>, <span class="NLM_publisher-loc">Boston</span>, <span class="NLM_year">2000</span>.</span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?hl=en&amp;publication_year=2000&amp;author=S.+A.+Vasquez&amp;author=V.+A.+Ivanov&amp;title=A+phase+coupled+method+for+solving+multiphase+problems+on+unstructured+mesh" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="

        </p><ol id="rightTab-references" class="useLabel"><li id="rightTab-ref1"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref1" class="rightTabRefNumLink">1</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit1" data-doi="10.1039/c2cs35307e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Strongly coupled inorganic–nano-carbon hybrid materials for energy storage</span>. <i>Chem. Soc.y Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>42</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3088</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3113</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2cs35307e</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Strongly coupled inorganic-nano-carbon hybrid materials for energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Hailiang; Dai, Hongjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">42</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3088-3113</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The global shift of energy prodn. from fossil fuels to renewable energy sources requires more efficient and reliable electrochem. energy storage devices.  In particular, the development of elec. or hydrogen powered vehicles calls for much-higher-performance batteries, supercapacitors and fuel cells than are currently available.  In this review, we present an approach to synthesize electrochem. energy storage materials to form strongly coupled hybrids (SC-hybrids) of inorg. nanomaterials and novel graphitic nano-carbon materials such as carbon nanotubes and graphene, through nucleation and growth of nanoparticles at the functional groups of oxidized graphitic nano-carbon.  We show that the inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials represent a new approach to synthesize electrode materials with higher electrochem. performance than traditional counterparts made by simple phys. mixts. of electrochem. active inorg. particles and conducting carbon materials.  The inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials are novel due to possible chem. bonding between inorg. nanoparticles and oxidized carbon, affording enhanced charge transport and increased rate capability of electrochem. materials without sacrificing specific capacity.  Nano-carbon with various degrees of oxidn. provides a novel substrate for nanoparticle nucleation and growth.  The interactions between inorg. precursors and oxidized-carbon substrates provide a degree of control over the morphol., size and structure of the resulting inorg. nanoparticles.  This paper reviews the recent development of inorg.-nano-carbon hybrid materials for electrochem. energy storage and conversion, including the prepn. and functionalization of graphene sheets and carbon nanotubes to impart oxygen contg. groups and defects, and methods of synthesis of nanoparticles of various morphologies on oxidized graphene and carbon nanotubes.  We then review the applications of the SC-hybrid materials for high performance lithium ion batteries, rechargeable Li-S and Li-O2 batteries, supercapacitors and ultrafast Ni-Fe batteries, and new electrocatalysts for oxygen redn., oxygen evolution and hydrogen evolution reactions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.pmatsci.2020.100717"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ciucci, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-based electrocatalysts for sustainable energy applications</span>. <i>Prog. Mater. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>116</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">100717</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Rapidly growing research interests are focused on carbon materials as electrocatalysts for oxygen redn. reaction (ORR), oxygen evolution reaction (OER), and hydrogen evolution reaction (HER), which are crit. for sustainable energy technologies including fuel cells, metal-air batteries, and water electrolyzers.  A no. of papers in this area have been published.  There is no systematic review yet covering all general methods to boost carbon-based electrocatalysts for ORR/OER/HER, and reporting their most recent progress.  To eliminate the gap, this contribution gives a timely and comprehensive review of such a rapidly growing field of great significance.  Brief introduction regarding the fundamentals of carbon-based ORR/OER/HER electrocatalysts and proceeds to summarize various synthesis methods of state-of-the-art carbon materials.  Most popular strategies to optimize electrocatalytic properties of carbons are thoroughly reviewed.  In addn. to metal-free carbons, advances regarding hybridized and self-supported carbon electrocatalysts are discussed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Metal chalcogenide thin films have a wide variety of applications and potential uses.  Tin(II) sulfide is one such material which presents a significant challenge with the need for high quality SnS, free of oxide materials (e.g., SnO2) and higher tin sulfides (e.g., Sn2S3 and SnS2).  This problem is compounded further when the target material exhibits a no. of polymorphic forms with different optoelectronic properties.  Unlike conventional chem. vapor deposition (CVD) and at. layer deposition (ALD), which rely heavily on having precursors that are volatile, stable, and reactive, the use of aerosol assisted CVD (AA-CVD) negates the need for volatile precursors.  We report here, for the first time, the novel and structurally characterized single source precursor (1), (dimethylamido)(N-phenyl-N',N'-dimethylthiouriate)tin(II) dimer, and its application in the deposition, by AA-CVD, of phase-pure films of SnS.  A mechanism for the oxidatively controlled formation of SnS from precursor 1 is also reported.  Significantly, thermal control of the deposition process allows for the unprecedented selective and exclusive formation of either orthorhombic-SnS (α-SnS) or zinc blende-SnS (ZB-SnS) polymorphs.  Thin films of α-SnS or ZB-SnS have been deposited onto Mo, fluorine doped tin oxide (FTO), Si, and glass substrates at the optimized deposition temps. of 375 and 300 °C, resp.  The densely packed polycryst. thin films have been characterized by X-ray diffraction, SEM, at. force microscopy, Raman spectroscopy, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy, and XPS anal.  These data confirmed the phase purity of the SnS formed.  Optical anal. of the α-SnS and ZB-SnS films shows distinctly different optical properties with direct band gaps of 1.34 and 1.78 eV, resp.  Furthermore, photoelectrochem. and external quantum efficiency (EQE) measurements were undertaken to assess the optoelectronic properties of the deposited samples.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The thermoelec. effect enables direct and reversible conversion between thermal and elec. energy, and provides a viable route for power generation from waste heat.  The efficiency of thermoelec. materials is dictated by the dimensionless figure of merit, ZT (where Z is the figure of merit and T is abs. temp.), which governs the Carnot efficiency for heat conversion.  Enhancements above the generally high threshold value of 2.5 have important implications for com. deployment, esp. for compds. free of Pb and Te.  Here we report an unprecedented ZT of 2.6 ± 0.3 at 923 K, realized in SnSe single crystals measured along the b axis of the room-temp. orthorhombic unit cell.  This material also shows a high ZT of 2.3 ± 0.3 along the c axis but a significantly reduced ZT of 0.8 ± 0.2 along the a axis.  We attribute the remarkably high ZT along the b axis to the intrinsically ultralow lattice thermal cond. in SnSe.  The layered structure of SnSe derives from a distorted rock-salt structure, and features anomalously high Grueneisen parameters, which reflect the anharmonic and anisotropic bonding.  We attribute the exceptionally low lattice thermal cond. (0.23 ± 0.03 W m-1 K-1 at 973 K) in SnSe to the anharmonicity.  These findings highlight alternative strategies to nanostructuring for achieving high thermoelec. performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C7RA00373K"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Improved thermoelectric properties of SnS synthesized by chemical precipitation</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">16795</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Using d. functional theory, the authors systematically investigate the lattice thermal cond. and carrier mobility of monolayer SnX2 (X = S, Se).  The room-temp. ultra low lattice thermal conductivities found in monolayer SnS2 (6.41 W m-1 K-1) and SnSe2 (3.82 W m-1 K-1) are attributed to the low phonon velocity, low Debye temp., weak bonding interactions, and strong anharmonicity in monolayer SnX2. Particularly appealing is their utilization as electrocatalysts for the synthesis of energy vectors and value-added chems. such as C-based chems. from the CO2 redn. reaction (CO2R) or ammonia from the nitrogen fixation reaction (NRR). For these two reactions, we comprehensively review recent progress in unveiling reaction mechanisms at different sites and the most effective strategies for fabricating catalysts that, by exploiting the structural and electronic peculiarities of transition metal chalcogenides, can outperform many metallic compds.  Transition metal chalcogenides outperform state-of-the-art catalysts for CO2 to CO redn. in ionic liqs. due to the favorable CO2 adsorption on the metal edge sites, whereas the basal sites, due to their conformation, represent an appealing design space for redn. of CO2 to complex carbon products.                 10.1002/sstr.202200396</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High Sodium-Ion Battery Capacity in Sulfur-Deficient Tin(II) Sulfide Thin Films with a Microrod Morphology</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Zening; Hyett, Geoffrey; Reid, Gillian; Robinson, Fred; Cibin, Giannantonio; Hector, Andrew L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Structures</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sulfur-deficient SnS thin films for sodium-ion battery anode application are prepd. using aerosol-assisted chem. vapor deposition.  Growth directly onto the metal foil current collector forms sulfur-deficient SnS microrod structures via a vapor-liq.-solid growth mechanism, with 92 nm av. SnS crystallite size and an 800 nm film thickness.  The sulfur deficiency is demonstrated with energy-dispersive X-ray anal., powder X-ray diffraction, and X-ray absorption near-edge structure analyses.  This sulfur-deficient SnS material demonstrates a very high capacity in sodium half cells.  The first redn. scan at a specific current of 150 mA g-1 shows a capacity of 1084 mAh g-1.  At the 50th cycle the specific capacity is 638 mAh g-1 for redn. and 593 mAh g-1 for oxidn.  This capacity is demonstrated for tin sulfide itself without the need for a nanostructured carbon support, unlike previous high capacity SnS anodes in the literature.  Both the capacity and ex situ characterization expts. indicate a conversion reaction producing tin, followed by alloying with sodium during redn., and that both of these processes are reversible during oxidn.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1016/0032-5910(73)80037-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Geldart, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Types of gas fluidization</span>. <i>Powder Technology</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1973</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">285</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Types of gas fluidization</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Geldart, D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Powder Technology</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">A study was made to characterize powders having broadly similar properties when fluidized by a gas.  The behavior of powders is characterized by 4 groups according to d. difference (ρs-ρf) and mean size.  Group A powders show dense-phase expansion after min. fluidization and prior to the commencement of bubbling.  Group B powders bubble at the min. fluidization velocity.  Group C is difficult to fluidize, while group D includes large size and/or d. and spouts readily.  A criterion distinguishing between groups A and B is given and agrees well with published data; it also predicts that a change in pressure and/or gas viscosity may cause a change in the behavior of particles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1007/s11051-012-0737-4"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van

                        rightTab-cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluidization of nanopowders: a review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">van Ommen, J. Ruud; Valverde, Jose Manuel; Pfeffer, Robert</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Nanoparticle Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Nanoparticles (NPs) are applied in a wide range of processes, and their use continues to increase.  Fluidization is one of the best techniques available to disperse and process NPs.  NPs cannot be fluidized individually; they fluidize as very porous agglomerates.  The objective of this article is to review the developments in nanopowder fluidization.  Often, it is needed to apply an assistance method, such as vibration or microjets, to obtain proper fluidization.  These methods can greatly improve the fluidization characteristics, strongly increase the bed expansion, and lead to a better mixing of the bed material.  Several approaches have been applied to model the behavior of fluidized nanopowders.  The av. size of fluidized NP agglomerates can be estd. using a force balance or by a modified Richardson and Zaki equation.  Some first attempts have been made to apply computational fluid dynamics.  Fluidization can also be used to provide individual NPs with a thin coating of another material and to mix two different species of nanopowder.  The application of nanopowder fluidization in practice is still limited, but a wide range of potential applications is foreseen.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1016/j.powtec.2020.12.011"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A review on fluidization of Geldart Group C powders through nanoparticle modulation</span>. <i>Powder Technology</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>381</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">698</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Gas-solid fluidization is a popular technique for processing these particles.  However, most Group C particles are not fluidizable due to strong interparticle forces.  In recent decades, one effective technique assisting their fluidization has been the addn. of small amts. of nanoparticles to reduce their interparticle forces.  This paper reviews both theor. and exptl. studies on the gas fluidization of Group C particles, including nanoparticle modulation techniques, surface morphol., and modeling of interparticle forces.  More importantly, the fluidization of these coated Group C particles (the so-called Group C+ particles) have been comprehensively discussed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Formation and modeling of aluminide diffusion coatings onto nickel and nickel alloys by a fluidized bed chem. vapor deposition (FBCVD) process are described.  In this process, the treated object is suspended within the FB reactor, which contains the treating agent.  The latter is a mixt. of powders of the donor of the coating-forming element (FexAly), and of a filler material (Al2O3).  As an activator of the process, a halide compd. is used (NH4Cl).  The bed is fluidized by inert gas (Ar).  The precursor vapors of the element to be deposited are formed in situ, by the reaction of the donor with the activator. The modeling of the nickel-aluminide coating growth on nickel, was based on the diffusion equations of aluminum in pure nickel, considering the intermetallic compds. predicted by the Ni-Al phase diagram.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This work reports exptl. results on the heat transfer between a fluidized bed of fine particles and a submerged surface. Dry air at ambient pressure and temp. was used as fluidizing gas.  Three different exchange surfaces, namely a sphere and two cylinders with different base diam. and same height, were used.                 10.1016/0022-0396(87)90038-6</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1017/S0022112084000586"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lun, C. K. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Savage, S. B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeffrey, D. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chepurniy, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Kinetic theories for granular flow: inelastic particles in couette flow and slightly inelastic particles in a general flow field</span>. <i>J. Fluid Mech.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1984</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>140</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">223</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1017/S0022112084000586</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chapman, S.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">Cowling, T. G.</span></span> <i>The Mathematical Theory of Non-Uniform Gases</i>, <span class="NLM_edition">3</span>rd edition, <span class="NLM_publisher-name">Cambridge University Press</span>, <span class="NLM_publisher-loc">Cambridge, UK</span>, <span class="NLM_year">1970</span>, pp.  <span class="NLM_fpage">119</span>.</span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1017/S0022112087000570"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johnson, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jackson, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Frictional-collisional constitutive relations for granular materials, with application to plane shearing</span>. <i>J. Fluid Mech.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1987</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>176</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">67</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1017/S0022112087000570</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Frictional-collisional constitutive relations for granular materials, with application to plane shearing</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Johnson, P. C.; Jackson, R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Fluid Mechanics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1987</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">176</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">67-93</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JFLSA7</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-1120</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">In a granular material, stress is transmitted by forces exerted at points of mutual contact between particles.  When the particles are close together and deformation of the assembly is slow, contacts are sustained for long times, and these forces consist of normal reactions and the assocd. tangential forces due to friction.  When the particles are widely spaced and deformation is rapid, on the other hand, contacts are brief and can be regarded as collisions, during which momentum is transferred.  While constitutive relations are available which model both these situations, in many cases the av. contact times lie between the 2 extremes.  Constitutive relations and boundary conditions are proposed for this intermediate case and to solve the corresponding equations of motion for plane shear of a cohesionless granular material between infinite horizontal plates.  In general, not all the material between the plates participates in shearing, and the solns. for the shearing material are coupled to a yield condition for the nonshearing material to give a complete soln. of the problem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vasquez, S. A.</span>; <span class="NLM_string-name">Ivanov, V. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A phase coupled method for solving multiphase problems on unstructured mesh</span>, In:  <i>Proceedings of ASME FEDSM’00: ASME 2000 Fluids Engineering Division Summer Meeting</i>, <span class="NLM_publisher-loc">Boston</span>, <span class="NLM_year">2000</span>.</span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i47">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline"><sup>1</sup>H and <sup>13</sup>C NMR spectra of [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuSe(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>Se<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] and [SnCl<sub>4</sub>{<sup><i>n</i></sup>BuS(CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>S<sup><i>n</i></sup>Bu}] precursors. XRD and SEM of SnSe<sub>2</sub> and SnS<sub>2</sub> thin films on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> substrates obtained by LP-CVD from single-source precursors; SEM and EDX spectra of tin dichalcogenide thin films on Si/SiO<sub>2</sub> tiles obtained by LP-CVD from single-source precursors; powder XRD and SEM of HC-SnSe<sub>2</sub> composites obtained by plug flow-CVD at different temperatures; TGA of composites synthesized by plug-flow CVD and FB-CVD in different conditions and powder XRD of the residue; CFD close up of the specific directional <i>y</i>-velocities along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.1 m/s inlet velocity at different times; positive and negative <i>y</i>-velocities within the bulb along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.4 m/s inlet velocity at varying fluidization times and comparison with the positive only <i>y</i>-velocities; close up of the positive and negative <i>y</i>-velocities along the <i>XY</i>-plane for the 0.4 m/s inlet velocity at 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5 s BET analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.4c00297/suppl_file/ao4c00297_si_001.pdf">ao4c00297_si_001.pdf (1.6 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>
-->

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="9318a7667950222a-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Omega";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
